<tr>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:left; text-size: 80%"><b>President before election</b><br/>
<p><a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a><br/>
<a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a></p>
</td>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:right; text-size: 80%"><b>Elected President</b><br/>
<p><a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a><br/>
<a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a></p>
</td>

</table>
<p>The <b>United States presidential election of 1860</b> was the 19th quadrennial <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election" title="United States presidential election">presidential election</a> to select the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President of the United States</a>. The election was held on Tuesday, November 6, 1860, and served as the immediate impetus for the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>.</p>
<p>The United States had been divided during the 1850s on questions surrounding the expansion of <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slavery</a> and the rights of slave owners. Incumbent President <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a>, like his predecessor <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a>, was a northerner with sympathies for the South. He recommended that Supreme Court Justice <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cooper_Grier" title="Robert Cooper Grier">Robert Grier</a> vote <a href="/wiki/Proslavery" title="Proslavery">proslavery</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Dred_Scott_v._Sandford" title="Dred Scott v. Sandford">Dred Scott v. Sandford</a></i> case of 1857. This was so unpopular it backfired on Buchanan’s presidency, allowing the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> to win a majority in the House in 1858 and full control of Congress in 1860. Buchanan declined to seek re-election. In 1860, these issues broke the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> into <a href="/wiki/Northern_United_States" title="Northern United States">Northern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern</a> factions, and a new <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Union_Party_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Union Party (United States)">Constitutional Union Party</a> appeared. In the face of a divided opposition, the newly created <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> (founded in 1854), secured a majority of the electoral votes, putting <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> with almost no support from the South.</p>
<p>Between Election Day and <a href="/wiki/First_inauguration_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="First inauguration of Abraham Lincoln">Lincoln’s inauguration</a>, seven slave-holding Southern states declared their <a href="/wiki/Secession_in_the_United_States" title="Secession in the United States">secession</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> and formed the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States of America</a>, with the new <a href="/wiki/Polity" title="Polity">polity</a>’s capital being placed in <a href="/wiki/Montgomery,_Alabama" title="Montgomery, Alabama">Montgomery, Alabama</a>. Military maneuvering between the new Confederacy and the United States, and disputes over US forts in Confederate territory led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firing_on_Fort_Sumter" title="Firing on Fort Sumter">firing on Fort Sumter</a>, which precipitated the civil war that lasted from 1861 to 1865.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nominations">Nominations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1860&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Nominations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The 1860 presidential election conventions were unusually tumultuous, due in particular to a split in the Democratic Party that led to rival conventions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Democratic_.28Northern_Democratic.29_Party_Nomination">Democratic (Northern Democratic) Party Nomination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1860&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Democratic (Northern Democratic) Party Nomination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:St_Andrew%27s_Hall,_Charleston.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="549" data-file-width="380" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/St_Andrew%27s_Hall%2C_Charleston.jpg/200px-St_Andrew%27s_Hall%2C_Charleston.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/St_Andrew%27s_Hall%2C_Charleston.jpg/300px-St_Andrew%27s_Hall%2C_Charleston.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/84/St_Andrew%27s_Hall%2C_Charleston.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:St_Andrew%27s_Hall,_Charleston.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The South Carolina Institute located in <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston</a>. The Institute hosted the <a href="/wiki/1860_Democratic_National_Conventions" title="1860 Democratic National Conventions">Democratic National Convention</a> and December Secession Convention in 1860.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1860_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1860 Democratic National Convention">1860 Democratic National Convention</a></div>
<p>Democratic candidates:</p>
<ul>
<li>Stephen Douglas, Senator from Illinois</li>
<li>James Guthrie, former Secretary Treasury</li>
<li>Robert Mercer Taliaferro Hunter, Senator from Virginia</li>
<li>Joseph Lane, Senator from Oregon</li>
<li>Daniel S. Dickinson, former Senator from New York</li>
<li>Andrew Johnson, Senator from Tennessee</li>
</ul>

<p>At the Democratic National Convention held in Institute Hall in <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston, South Carolina</a>, in April 1860, 51 Southern Democrats walked out over a platform dispute. The extreme pro-slavery “<a href="/wiki/Fire-Eaters" title="Fire-Eaters">Fire-Eater</a>” <a href="/wiki/William_Lowndes_Yancey" title="William Lowndes Yancey">William Lowndes Yancey</a> and the Alabama delegation first left the hall, followed by the delegates of Florida, Georgia, Louisiana, Mississippi, South Carolina, Texas, three of the four delegates from Arkansas, and one of the three delegates from Delaware.</p>
<p>Six candidates were nominated: <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas" title="Stephen A. Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a> from Illinois, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Guthrie_(American_politician)" title="James Guthrie (American politician)">James Guthrie</a> from Kentucky, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Mercer_Taliaferro_Hunter" title="Robert Mercer Taliaferro Hunter">Robert Mercer Taliaferro Hunter</a> from Virginia, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lane" title="Joseph Lane">Joseph Lane</a> from Oregon, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_S._Dickinson" title="Daniel S. Dickinson">Daniel S. Dickinson</a> from New York, and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a> from Tennessee. Three other candidates, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Toucey" title="Isaac Toucey">Isaac Toucey</a> from Connecticut, <a href="/wiki/James_Pearce" title="James Pearce">James Pearce</a> from Maryland, and <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Davis" title="Jefferson Davis">Jefferson Davis</a> from Mississippi (the future <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Confederate_States_of_America" title="President of the Confederate States of America">president of the Confederate States</a>) also received votes. Douglas, a moderate on the slavery issue who favored “<a href="/wiki/Popular_sovereignty_in_the_United_States" title="Popular sovereignty in the United States">popular sovereignty</a>”, was ahead on the first ballot, but needed 56.5 more votes to secure the nomination. On the 57th ballot, Douglas was still ahead, but 51.5 votes short of nomination. In desperation, the delegates agreed on May 3 to stop voting and adjourn the convention.</p>
<p>The Democrats convened again at the Front Street Theater in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore,_Maryland" title="Baltimore, Maryland">Baltimore, Maryland</a>, on June 18. This time, 110 Southern delegates (led by “Fire-Eaters”) walked out when the convention would not adopt a resolution supporting extending slavery into territories whose voters did not want it. Some considered <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Seymour" title="Horatio Seymour">Horatio Seymour</a> a compromise candidate for the National Democratic nomination at the reconvening convention in Baltimore. Seymour wrote a letter to the editor of his local newspaper declaring unreservedly that he was not a candidate for either spot on the ticket. After two ballots, the remaining Democrats nominated the ticket of <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas" title="Stephen A. Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a> from Illinois for president. <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Fitzpatrick" title="Benjamin Fitzpatrick">Benjamin Fitzpatrick</a> from Alabama was nominated for vice president, but he refused the nomination. That nomination ultimately went instead to <a href="/wiki/Herschel_Vespasian_Johnson" title="Herschel Vespasian Johnson">Herschel Vespasian Johnson</a> from Georgia.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Democratic_Party_Nomination">Southern Democratic Party Nomination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1860&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Southern Democratic Party Nomination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MDINST.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="493" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/MDINST.jpg/200px-MDINST.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/MDINST.jpg/300px-MDINST.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/MDINST.jpg/400px-MDINST.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MDINST.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Maryland Institute Hall, Baltimore Here bolting delegates nominated Breckinridge before Richmond vote<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FreehlingWilliam_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FreehlingWilliam-3">[3]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1860_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1860 Democratic National Convention">1860 Democratic National Convention</a></div>
<p>Southern Democratic candidates:</p>
<ul>
<li>John C. Breckinridge, Vice President of the United States</li>
<li>Daniel S. Dickinson, former Senator from New York</li>
<li>Robert Mercer Taliaferro Hunter, Senator from Virginia</li>
<li>Joseph Lane, Senator from Oregon</li>
<li>Jefferson Davis, Senator from Mississippi</li>
</ul>

<p>The Charleston bolters reconvened in <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond, Virginia</a> on June 11. When the Democrats reconvened in Baltimore, they rejoined (except South Carolina and Florida, who stayed in Richmond).</p>
<p>When the convention seated two replacement delegations on June 18, they bolted again, now accompanied by nearly all other Southern delegates, as well as erstwhile Convention chair <a href="/wiki/Caleb_Cushing" title="Caleb Cushing">Caleb Cushing</a>, a New Englander and former member of <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a>’s cabinet. This larger group met immediately in Baltimore's Institute Hall, with Cushing again presiding. They adopted the pro-slavery platform rejected at Charleston, and nominated <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President</a> <a href="/wiki/John_C._Breckinridge" title="John C. Breckinridge">John C. Breckinridge</a> for President, and Senator <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lane" title="Joseph Lane">Joseph Lane</a> from Oregon for Vice President.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Yancey and some (less than half) of the bolters, almost entirely from the Lower South, met on June 28 in Richmond, along with the South Carolina and Florida delegations. This convention affirmed the nominations of Breckinridge and Lane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FreehlingWilliam_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FreehlingWilliam-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Besides the Democratic Parties in the southern states, the Breckinridge/Lane ticket was also supported by the Buchanan administration. Buchanan’s own continued prestige in his home state of <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> ensured that Breckinridge would be the principal Democratic candidate in that populous state. Breckinridge was the last sitting Vice President nominated for President until <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1960" title="United States presidential election, 1960">1960</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Republican_Party_Nomination">Republican Party Nomination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1860&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Republican Party Nomination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chicago_Wigwam.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="959" data-file-width="1395" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Chicago_Wigwam.png/230px-Chicago_Wigwam.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Chicago_Wigwam.png/345px-Chicago_Wigwam.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Chicago_Wigwam.png/460px-Chicago_Wigwam.png 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chicago_Wigwam.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Wigwam_(Chicago)" title="Wigwam (Chicago)">Chicago Wigwam</a>, Republican Convention</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1860_Republican_National_Convention" title="1860 Republican National Convention">1860 Republican National Convention</a></div>
<p>Republican candidates:</p>
<ul>
<li>Abraham Lincoln, former representative from Illinois</li>
<li>William Seward, Senator from New York</li>
<li>Simon Cameron, Senator from Pennsylvania</li>
<li>Salmon P. Chase, Governor of Ohio</li>
<li>Edward Bates, former representative from Missouri</li>
<li>John McLean, Associate Justice of the U.S. Supreme Court</li>
<li>Benjamin Wade, Senator from Ohio</li>
<li>William L. Dayton, former Senator from New Jersey</li>
</ul>

<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_L._Dayton.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2285" data-file-width="1717" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/William_L._Dayton.jpg/180px-William_L._Dayton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/William_L._Dayton.jpg/270px-William_L._Dayton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/William_L._Dayton.jpg/361px-William_L._Dayton.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senator</a> <b><a href="/wiki/William_L._Dayton" title="William L. Dayton">William L. Dayton</a></b> from <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The Republican National Convention met in mid-May 1860, after the Democrats had been forced to adjourn their convention in Charleston. With the Democrats in disarray and a sweep of the Northern states possible, the Republicans felt confident going into their convention in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>. <a href="/wiki/William_H._Seward" title="William H. Seward">William H. Seward</a> from New York was considered the front runner, followed by <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> from Illinois, <a href="/wiki/Salmon_P._Chase" title="Salmon P. Chase">Salmon P. Chase</a> from Ohio, and Missouri's <a href="/wiki/Edward_Bates" title="Edward Bates">Edward Bates</a>.</p>
<p>As the convention developed, however, it was revealed that Seward, Chase, and Bates had each alienated factions of the Republican Party. Delegates were concerned that Seward was too closely identified with the radical wing of the party, and his moves toward the center had alienated the radicals. Chase, a former Democrat, had alienated many of the former Whigs by his coalition with the Democrats in the late 1840s. He had also opposed tariffs demanded by Pennsylvania, and critically, had opposition from his own delegation from Ohio. Bates outlined his positions on the extension of slavery into the territories and equal constitutional rights for all citizens, positions that alienated his supporters in the border states and Southern conservatives. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_American" title="German American">German Americans</a> in the party opposed Bates because of his past association with the <a href="/wiki/Know_Nothing" title="Know Nothing">Know Nothings</a>.</p>
<p>Since it was essential to carry the West, and because Lincoln had a national reputation from his debates and speeches as the most articulate moderate, he won the party's nomination for president on the third ballot on May 18, 1860. Senator <a href="/wiki/Hannibal_Hamlin" title="Hannibal Hamlin">Hannibal Hamlin</a> from Maine was nominated for vice-president, defeating <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Marcellus_Clay_(politician)" title="Cassius Marcellus Clay (politician)">Cassius Clay</a> from Kentucky.</p>
<p>The party platform<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> promised not to interfere with slavery in the states, but suggested an opposition to slavery in the territories. The platform promised tariffs protecting industry and workers, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homestead_Act" title="Homestead Act">Homestead Act</a> granting free farmland in the West to settlers, and the funding of a <a href="/wiki/Transcontinental_railroad" title="Transcontinental railroad">transcontinental railroad</a>. There was no mention of Mormonism (which had been condemned in the Party’s 1856 platform), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fugitive_Slave_Act" title="Fugitive Slave Act">Fugitive Slave Act</a>, personal liberty laws, or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dred_Scott_decision" title="Dred Scott decision">Dred Scott decision</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> While the Seward forces were disappointed at the nomination of a little-known western upstart, they rallied behind Lincoln. Abolitionists, however, were angry at the selection of a moderate and had little faith in Lincoln.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baum1984_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baum1984-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitutional_Union_Party_Nomination">Constitutional Union Party Nomination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1860&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Constitutional Union Party Nomination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bell-everett-campaign-poster-1860b.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="475" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Bell-everett-campaign-poster-1860b.jpg/170px-Bell-everett-campaign-poster-1860b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Bell-everett-campaign-poster-1860b.jpg/255px-Bell-everett-campaign-poster-1860b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Bell-everett-campaign-poster-1860b.jpg/340px-Bell-everett-campaign-poster-1860b.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bell-everett-campaign-poster-1860b.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Constitutional Union campaign poster, 1860, portraying John Bell and Edward Everett, respectively the candidates for President and Vice-President. Once Lincoln was inaugurated, and called up the militia, Bell supported the secession of Tennessee. In 1863, Everett dedicated the new cemetery at Gettysburg.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1860_Constitutional_Union_Convention" title="1860 Constitutional Union Convention">1860 Constitutional Union Convention</a></div>
<p>Constitutional Union candidates:</p>
<ul>
<li>John Bell, former Senator from Tennessee</li>
<li>Sam Houston, Governor of Texas</li>
<li>John J. Crittenden, Senator from Kentucky</li>
<li>Edward Everett, former Senator from Massachusetts</li>
<li>William A. Graham, former Senator from North Carolina</li>
<li>William C. Rives, former Senator from Virginia</li>
</ul>

</div>

<p>The <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Union_Party_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Union Party (United States)">Constitutional Union Party</a> was formed by remnants of both the defunct <a href="/wiki/Know_Nothing" title="Know Nothing">Know Nothing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Parties</a> who were unwilling to join either the Republicans or the Democrats. The parties members hoped to stave off Southern secession by avoiding the slavery issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schulten20101110_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-schulten20101110-9">[9]</a></sup> They met in the Eastside District Courthouse of Baltimore and nominated <a href="/wiki/John_Bell_(Tennessee_politician)" title="John Bell (Tennessee politician)">John Bell</a> from Tennessee for president over <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Texas" title="List of Governors of Texas">Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Sam_Houston" title="Sam Houston">Sam Houston</a> of Texas on the second ballot. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Everett" title="Edward Everett">Edward Everett</a> was nominated for vice-president at the convention on May 9, 1860, one week before Lincoln.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lossing1866_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-lossing1866-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>John Bell was a former Whig who had opposed the <a href="/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska_Act" title="Kansas–Nebraska Act">Kansas–Nebraska Act</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lecompton_Constitution" title="Lecompton Constitution">Lecompton Constitution</a>. Edward Everett had been president of <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> in the Fillmore administration. The party platform advocated compromise to save the Union with the slogan “The Union as it is, and the Constitution as it is.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberty_.28Union.29_Party_nomination">Liberty (Union) Party nomination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1860&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Liberty (Union) Party nomination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Liberty (Union) candidates:</p>
<ul>
<li>Gerrit Smith, former representative from New York</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberty_.28Union.29_candidates_gallery">Liberty (Union) candidates gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1860&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Liberty (Union) candidates gallery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed" style="text-align:center">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 127.33333333333px">
<div style="width: 127.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 125.33333333333px;">
<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GerritSmith-1840s.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="792" data-file-width="620" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/GerritSmith-1840s.jpg/188px-GerritSmith-1840s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/GerritSmith-1840s.jpg/282px-GerritSmith-1840s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c1/GerritSmith-1840s.jpg/376px-GerritSmith-1840s.jpg 2x" width="126"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Former <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">Representative</a> <b><a href="/wiki/Gerrit_Smith" title="Gerrit Smith">Gerrit Smith</a></b> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The Liberty Party as formed in 1860 was a splinter (or remnant) of the former <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Party_(United_States,_1840)" title="Liberty Party (United States, 1840)">Liberty Party of the 1840s</a>, after most of its membership left to join the <a href="/wiki/Free_Soil_Party" title="Free Soil Party">Free Soil Party</a> in 1848 and nearly all of what remained of it joined the Republicans in 1854. A convention of 100 delegates was held in Convention Hall, Syracuse, New York, on August 29, 1860. Delegates were in attendance from New York, Pennsylvania, New Jersey, Michigan, Illinois, Ohio, Kentucky, and Massachusetts. Several of the delegates were women.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gerrit_Smith" title="Gerrit Smith">Gerrit Smith</a>, a prominent abolitionist and the <a href="/wiki/1848_Free_Soil_%26_Liberty_national_Conventions" title="1848 Free Soil &amp; Liberty national Conventions">1848 presidential nominee of the original Liberty Party</a>, had sent a letter in which he stated that his health had been so poor that he had not been able to be away from home since 1858. Nonetheless, he remained popular in the party because he had helped inspire some of <a href="/wiki/John_Brown_(abolitionist)" title="John Brown (abolitionist)">John Brown's</a> supporters at the <a href="/wiki/John_Brown%27s_raid_on_Harpers_Ferry" title="John Brown's raid on Harpers Ferry">Raid on Harpers Ferry</a>. In his letter, Smith donated $50 to pay for the printing of ballots in the various states.</p>
<p>There was quite a spirited contest between the friends of Gerrit Smith and <a href="/wiki/William_Goodell_(abolitionist)" title="William Goodell (abolitionist)">William Goodell</a> in regard to the nomination for the presidency. In spite of his professed ill health, Gerrit Smith was nominated for president and Samuel McFarland from Pennsylvania was nominated for vice president.</p>
<p>In Ohio, a slate of presidential electors pledged to Smith ran with the name of the Union Party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="People.27s_Party_nomination">People's Party nomination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1860&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: People's Party nomination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed" style="text-align:center">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 110.66666666667px">
<div style="width: 110.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 108.66666666667px;">
<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SHouston_2.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="754" data-file-width="512" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/SHouston_2.jpg/163px-SHouston_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/SHouston_2.jpg/244px-SHouston_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/SHouston_2.jpg/326px-SHouston_2.jpg 2x" width="109"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Texas" title="Governor of Texas">Governor</a> <b><a href="/wiki/Sam_Houston" title="Sam Houston">Sam Houston</a></b> of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The People's Party was a loose association of the supporters of Governor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuel_Houston" title="Samuel Houston">Samuel Houston</a>. On April 20, 1860, the party held what it termed a national convention to nominate Houston for president on the <a href="/wiki/San_Jacinto_Battleground_State_Historic_Site" title="San Jacinto Battleground State Historic Site">San Jacinto Battlefield</a> in <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>. Houston's supporters at the gathering did not nominate a vice-presidential candidate, since they expected later gatherings to carry out that function. Later mass meetings were held in northern cities, such as New York City on May 30, 1860, but they too failed to nominate a vice-presidential candidate. Houston withdrew from the race on August 16, convinced that his candidacy would only make it easier for the Republican candidate to win, and urged the formation of a Unified "Union" ticket in opposition to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="An_election_for_disunion">An election for disunion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1860&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: An election for disunion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li><a href="/wiki/John_Brown%27s_raid_on_Harpers_Ferry" title="John Brown's raid on Harpers Ferry">Brown's raid on Harpers Ferry</a></li>
<li><strong class="selflink">1860 presidential election</strong></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Crittenden_Compromise" title="Crittenden Compromise">Crittenden Compromise</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America#Seceding_states" title="Confederate States of America">Secession of Southern States</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Star_of_the_West" title="Star of the West">Star of the West</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Corwin_Amendment" title="Corwin Amendment">Corwin Amendment</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Sumter" title="Battle of Fort Sumter">Battle of Fort Sumter</a></li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Events_leading_to_American_Civil_War" title="Template:Events leading to American Civil War"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Events_leading_to_American_Civil_War" title="Template talk:Events leading to American Civil War"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Events_leading_to_American_Civil_War&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In their campaigning, Bell and Douglas both claimed that disunion would not necessarily follow a Lincoln election. Nonetheless, loyal army officers in Virginia, Kansas and South Carolina warned Lincoln of military preparations to the contrary. Secessionists threw their support behind Breckinridge in an attempt either to force the anti-Republican candidates to coordinate their electoral votes or throw the election into the House of Representatives, where the selection of the president would be made by the representatives elected in 1858, before the Republican majorities in both House and Senate achieved in 1860 were seated in the new <a href="/wiki/37th_United_States_Congress" title="37th United States Congress">37th Congress</a>. Mexican War hero <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott" title="Winfield Scott">Winfield Scott</a> suggested to Lincoln that he assume the powers of a commander-in-chief before inauguration. But historian Bruce Chadwick observes that Lincoln and his advisors ignored the widespread alarms and threats of secession as mere election trickery.</p>
<p>Indeed, voting in the South was not as monolithic as the Electoral College map would make it seem. Economically, culturally, and politically, the South was made up of three regions. In the states of the “Upper” South, also known as the “<a href="/wiki/Border_states_(American_Civil_War)" title="Border states (American Civil War)">Border States</a>” (Delaware, Maryland, Kentucky, and Missouri along with the Kansas territories), unionist popular votes were scattered among Lincoln, Douglas, and Bell, to form a majority in all four. In four of the five “Middle” South states, there was a unionist majority divided between Douglas and Bell in Virginia and Tennessee; in North Carolina and Arkansas, the unionist (Bell and Douglas) vote approached a majority. Texas was the only Middle South state that Breckinridge carried convincingly. In three of the six “Deep” South, unionists (Bell and Douglas) won divided majorities in Georgia and Louisiana or neared it in Alabama. Breckinridge convincingly carried only three of the six states of the Deep South (South Carolina, Florida, and Mississippi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HarpWeek_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-HarpWeek-16">[16]</a></sup> These three Deep South states were all among the four Southern states with the lowest white populations; together, they held only nine-percent of Southern whites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the states that would become the Confederacy, the three states with the highest voter turnouts voted the most one-sided. Texas, with five percent of the total wartime South's population, voted 80 percent Breckinridge. Kentucky and Missouri, with one-fourth the total population, voted 68 percent pro-union Bell, Douglas and Lincoln. In comparison, the six states of the Deep South making up one-fourth the Confederate voting population, split 57 percent Breckinridge versus 43 percent for the three pro-union candidates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[nb 1]</a></sup> The four states that were admitted to the Confederacy after Fort Sumter held almost half its population, and voted a narrow combined majority of 53 percent for the pro-union candidates.</p>
<p>In the eleven states that would later declare their secession from the Union and be controlled by Confederate armies, ballots for Lincoln were cast only in Virginia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[nb 2]</a></sup> where he received only 1.1 percent of the popular vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HarpWeek_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-HarpWeek-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> In order to distribute ballots in a state, candidates needed citizens in that state who would pledge to vote for the candidate in the Electoral College. In ten southern slave states, no citizens would publicly pledge such support for Lincoln.</p>
<p>In the four slave states that did not secede (Missouri, Kentucky, Maryland, and Delaware), Lincoln came in fourth in every state except Delaware (where he finished third). Within the fifteen slave states, Lincoln won only two counties out of 996, Missouri’s <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_County,_Missouri" title="St. Louis County, Missouri">St. Louis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gasconade_County,_Missouri" title="Gasconade County, Missouri">Gasconade Counties</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HarpWeek_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-HarpWeek-16">[16]</a></sup> In the 1856 election, the Republican candidate for president had received no votes at all in ten of the fourteen slave states with a popular vote.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Results">Results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1860&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">

the Capitol, March 4, 1861</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1860.svg"><img alt="1860 Electoral College map with 35 states" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="1020" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/ElectoralCollege1860.svg/220px-ElectoralCollege1860.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/ElectoralCollege1860.svg/330px-ElectoralCollege1860.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/ElectoralCollege1860.svg/440px-ElectoralCollege1860.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div>

by Electoral College vote</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:602px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PresidentialCounty1860Colorbrewer.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="2800" height="364" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/PresidentialCounty1860Colorbrewer.gif/600px-PresidentialCounty1860Colorbrewer.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/PresidentialCounty1860Colorbrewer.gif/900px-PresidentialCounty1860Colorbrewer.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/PresidentialCounty1860Colorbrewer.gif/1200px-PresidentialCounty1860Colorbrewer.gif 2x" width="600"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PresidentialCounty1860Colorbrewer.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Results by county explicitly indicating the percentage for the winning candidate. Shades of red are for Lincoln (Republican), shades of blue are for Douglas (Northern Democratic), shades of green are for Breckinridge (Southern Democratic), shades of yellow are for Bell (Constitutional Union), and shades of purple are for “Fusion” (Non-Republican/Democratic Fusion). South Carolina had no popular vote.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The election was held on Tuesday, November 6, 1860, and was noteworthy for exaggerated sectionalism in a country that was soon to dissolve into civil war. <a href="/wiki/Voter_turnout" title="Voter turnout">Voter turnout</a> was 81.2%, the <a href="/wiki/Voter_turnout_in_the_United_States_presidential_elections" title="Voter turnout in the United States presidential elections">highest in American history</a> up to that time, and the second-highest overall (exceeded only in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1876" title="United States presidential election, 1876">election of 1876</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turnout_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-turnout-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census.gov_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-census.gov-24">[22]</a></sup> All six Presidents elected since <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> won re-election in 1832 had been one-term presidents, the last four with a popular vote under 51 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> Lincoln won the Electoral College with less than 40 percent of the popular vote nationwide by carrying states above the <a href="/wiki/Mason%E2%80%93Dixon_line" title="Mason–Dixon line">Mason–Dixon line</a> and north of the Ohio River, plus the states of California and Oregon in the Far West. Unlike every preceding president-elect, Lincoln did not carry even one slave state, and indeed he was not on the ballot in the southern states of North and South Carolina, Georgia, Florida, Alabama, Tennessee, Mississippi, Arkansas, Louisiana and Texas. He was the first President-elect to not be on the ballot in all states, a feat which has since been equalled thrice<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[nb 3]</a></sup> but never to the same extent.</p>
<p>The Republican victory resulted from the concentration of votes in the free states, which together controlled a majority of the presidential electors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup> Lincoln’s strategy was deliberately focused, in collaboration with Republican Party Chairman <a href="/wiki/Thurlow_Weed" title="Thurlow Weed">Thurlow Weed</a>, “Find ‘em and vote ‘em.” and based on expanding on the states Fremont won four years earlier. New York was critical with 35 Electoral College votes, 11.5 percent of the total; with Pennsylvania (27) and Ohio (23), a candidate could collect more than half (85) of the votes needed. The <a href="/wiki/Wide_Awakes" title="Wide Awakes">Wide Awakes</a> young Republican men’s organization massively expanded registered voter lists, and although Lincoln was not even on the ballot in many southern states, population increases in the free states had far exceeded those seen in the slave states for many years before the election of 1860, hence their dominance in the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The split in the Democratic party is sometimes held responsible for Lincoln’s victory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup> but he would still have won in the Electoral College, 169 to 134, if all of the anti-Lincoln voters had united behind a single candidate. Only in California, Oregon, and Illinois was Lincoln’s victory margin less than seven percent. In New England, he won every county.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>At most, a single opponent nationwide would only have deprived Lincoln of California and Oregon (both of which he only won via a plurality of the statewide vote), whose combined total of seven electoral votes would have made no difference to the result; every other state won by the Republicans was won by a clear majority of the vote. In the three states of New York, Rhode Island, and New Jersey where anti-Lincoln votes did combine into <a href="/wiki/Electoral_fusion" title="Electoral fusion">fusion tickets</a>, Lincoln still won two and split the electoral vote of New Jersey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup> If the opposition had formed fusion tickets in every state, Lincoln still would have received 169 electoral votes via-à-vis the 152 he required to win the Electoral College. Moreover, it is widely acknowledged that it was impossible for Lincoln’s opponents to combine because they hated each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The fractured Democratic vote did tip California, Oregon, and four New Jersey<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup> electoral votes to Lincoln, giving him 180 Electoral College votes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like Lincoln, Breckinridge and Bell won no electoral votes outside of their respective sections. While Bell retired to his family business, quietly supporting his state’s secession, Breckinridge served as a Confederate general. He finished second in the Electoral College with 72 votes, carrying eleven of fifteen slave states (including South Carolina, whose electors were chosen by the state legislature, not popular vote). Breckinridge stood a distant third in national popular vote at eighteen percent, but accrued 50–75 percent in the first seven states that would become the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States of America</a>. He took nine of the eleven states that eventually joined, plus the border slave states of Delaware and Maryland, losing only Virginia and Tennessee. Breckinridge received very little support in the free states, showing some strength only in California, Oregon, Pennsylvania and Connecticut.</p>
<p>Bell carried three slave states (Tennessee, Kentucky, and Virginia) and lost Maryland by only 722 votes. Nevertheless, he finished a remarkable second in all slave states won by Breckinridge and Douglas. He won 45 to 47 percent in Maryland, Tennessee and North Carolina and canvassed respectably with 36 to 40 percent in Missouri, Arkansas, Louisiana, Georgia, and Florida. Nonetheless, he came in last in the national popular vote at 12 percent.</p>
<p>Douglas was the only candidate who won electoral votes in both slave and free states (free New Jersey and slave Missouri). His support was the most widespread geographically; he finished second behind Lincoln in the popular vote with 29.5 percent, but last in the Electoral College. Douglas attained a 28–47 percent share in the states of the Mid-Atlantic, Midwest and Trans-Mississippi West, but slipped to 19–39 percent in New England. Outside his regional section, Douglas took 15–17 percent of the popular vote total in the slave states of Kentucky, Alabama and Louisiana, then 10 percent or less in the nine remaining slave states. Douglas, in his “Norfolk Doctrine”, reiterated in North Carolina, promised to keep the Union together by coercion if states proceeded to secede. The popular vote for Lincoln and Douglas combined was seventy percent of the turnout.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trigger_for_the_Civil_War">Trigger for the Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1860&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Trigger for the Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The election of Abraham Lincoln in 1860 was the immediate cause of southern resolutions of secession. He was the nominee of the Republican party with an anti-slavery expansion platform, he refused to acknowledge the right to secession, and he would not yield federal property within Southern states. Numerous historians have explored the reasons so many white Southerners adopted secessionism in 1860.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[33]</a></sup> Bertram Wyatt-Brown argues that secessionists desired independence as necessary for their honor. They could no longer tolerate northern attitudes that regarded slave ownership as a great sin and Northern politicians who insisted on stopping the spread of slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[35]</a></sup> Avery Craven argues that secessionists believed Lincoln's election meant long-term doom for their peculiar social system. These terms placed issues beyond the democratic process, and they placed "the great masses of men, North and South, helpless before the drift into war".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[36]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1860&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">“Deep South” here in presidential popular votes refers to Georgia, Florida, Alabama, Mississippi and Louisiana. It excludes South Carolina from the calculation, because in 1860 it chose presidential electors in the state legislature, without a popular vote.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ballots were printed sheets, usually printed by the party, with the name of the candidate(s) and the names of presidential electors who were pledged to that presidential candidate. Voters brought the ballot to the polling station and dropped it publicly into the election box. In order to receive any votes, a candidate (or his party) had to have ballots printed and organize a group of electors pledged to that candidate. Except in some border areas, the Republican party did not attempt any organization in the South and did not print ballots there because almost no one was willing to acknowledge publicly they were voting for Lincoln for fear of violent retribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1892" title="United States presidential election, 1892">1892</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> was not on the ballot in Colorado, Idaho, Kansas, North Dakota, or Wyoming, whilst neither <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harry_Truman" title="Harry Truman">Harry Truman</a> in 1948 nor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_Johnson" title="Lyndon Johnson">Lyndon Johnson</a> in 1964 were on the ballot in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election_in_Alabama,_1964" title="United States presidential election in Alabama, 1964">Alabama</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">4 of the electors pledged to Lincoln were elected since the Breckinridge and Bell electors finished behind all other candidates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._187_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._187-34">[31]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The 3 Douglas electors were elected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._187_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._187-34">[31]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Fusion vote used here is the vote for the high elector on the slate, who was pledged to Douglas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._187_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._187-34">[31]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Fusion slate consisted of 3 electors pledged to Douglas, and 2 each to Breckinridge and Bell. Nonetheless, different electors appeared in some counties for Breckinridge and Bell, resulting in lower totals for them and a split electoral outcome. The 3 Douglas electors were elected and 4 of those pledged to Lincoln. The Breckinridge and Bell electors finished behind all other candidates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._187_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._187-34">[31]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The slate of electors were pledged to 3 different candidates: 18 to Douglas, 10 to Bell, and 7 to Breckinridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._187_34-4"><a href="#cite_note-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._187-34">[31]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Not all of the Douglas supporters agreed to the Reading slate deal and established a separate Douglas only ticket. This slate comprised the 12 Douglas electoral candidates on the Reading ticket, and 15 additional Douglas supporters. This ticket was usually referred to as the Straight Douglas ticket. Thus 12 electoral candidates appeared on 2 tickets, Reading and Straight Douglas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._188_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._188-40">[32]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This vote is listed under the Fusion column, not the Breckinridge column as many other sources do, because this ticket was pledged to either of two candidates based on the national result. Additionally, the slate was almost equally divided between the supporters of Breckinridge and Douglas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._188_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._188-40">[32]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Democratic Party chose its slate of electors before the National Convention in Charleston, SC. Since this was decided before the party split, both Douglas supporters and Breckinridge supporters claimed the right for their man to be considered the party candidate and the support of the electoral slate. Eventually, the state party worked out an agreement: if either candidate could win the national election with Pennsylvania's electoral vote, then all her electoral votes would go to that candidate. Of the 27 electoral candidates, 15 were Breckinridge supporters; the remaining 12 were for Douglas. This was often referred to as the Reading electoral slate, because it was in that city that the state party chose it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._188_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._188-40">[32]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Douglas ticket in Rhode Island was supported by Breckinridge and Bell supporters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._188_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dubin.2C_Michael_J._2002.2C_p._188-40">[32]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li><a href="/wiki/Roy_Franklin_Nichols" title="Roy Franklin Nichols">Nichols, Roy Franklin</a>. <i>The Disruption of American Democracy</i> (1948), pp. 348–506, focused on the Democratic party</li>
<li>Parks, Joseph Howard. <i>John Bell of Tennessee</i> (1950), standard biography</li>
<li><cite class="citation book" id=".22Potter.22"><a href="/wiki/David_M._Potter" title="David M. Potter">Potter, David M.</a> (1976). <i><a href="/wiki/The_Impending_Crisis,_1848%E2%80%931861" title="The Impending Crisis, 1848–1861">The impending crisis, 1848–1861</a></i>. HarperCollins. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-06-131929-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-06-131929-7">978-0-06-131929-7</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AUnited+States+presidential+election%2C+1860&amp;rft.aufirst=David+M.&amp;rft.aulast=Potter&amp;rft.btitle=The+impending+crisis%2C+1848%931861&amp;rft.date=1976&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-06-131929-7&amp;rft.pub=HarperCollins&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/James_Ford_Rhodes" title="James Ford Rhodes">Rhodes, James Ford</a> (1920). <a class="external text" href="http://www.questia.com/PM.qst?a=o&amp;d=72303423" rel="nofollow"><i>History of the United States from the Compromise of 1850 to the McKinley-Bryan Campaign of 1896</i></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AUnited+States+presidential+election%2C+1860&amp;rft.aufirst=James+Ford&amp;rft.aulast=Rhodes&amp;rft.btitle=History+of+the+United+States+from+the+Compromise+of+1850+to+the+McKinley-Bryan+Campaign+of+1896&amp;rft.date=1920&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.questia.com%2FPM.qst%3Fa%3Do%26d%3D72303423&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> vol. 2, ch. 11. highly detailed narrative covering 1856–60</li>
